<ul><li>an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_ultrasonography" title="Medical ultrasonography">ultrasound/doppler study</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Carotid_artery" title="Carotid artery">carotid arteries</a> (to detect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carotid_stenosis" title="Carotid stenosis">carotid stenosis</a>) or dissection of the precerebral arteries;</li>
<li>an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrocardiogram" title="Electrocardiogram">electrocardiogram</a> (ECG) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Echocardiogram" title="Echocardiogram">echocardiogram</a> (to identify <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrhythmia" title="Cardiac arrhythmia">arrhythmias</a> and resultant clots in the heart which may spread to the brain vessels through the bloodstream);</li>
<li>a <a href="/wiki/Holter_monitor" title="Holter monitor">Holter monitor</a> study to identify intermittent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_arrhythmia" title="Heart arrhythmia">abnormal heart rhythms</a>;</li>
<li>an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiogram" title="Angiogram">angiogram</a> of the cerebral vasculature (if a bleed is thought to have originated from an <a href="/wiki/Aneurysm" title="Aneurysm">aneurysm</a> or <a href="/wiki/Arteriovenous_malformation" title="Arteriovenous malformation">arteriovenous malformation</a>);</li>
<li>blood tests to determine if <a href="/wiki/Hypercholesterolemia" title="Hypercholesterolemia">blood cholesterol is high</a>, if there is an abnormal <a href="/wiki/Bleeding_diathesis" title="Bleeding diathesis">tendency to bleed</a>, and if some rarer processes such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homocysteinuria" title="Homocysteinuria">homocystinuria</a> might be involved.</li></ul>
<p>For hemorrhagic strokes, a <a href="/wiki/Computed_tomography_angiography" title="Computed tomography angiography">CT</a> or <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_angiography" title="Magnetic resonance angiography">MRI scan with intravascular contrast</a> may be able to identify abnormalities in the brain arteries (such as aneurysms) or other sources of bleeding, and structural MRI if this shows no cause. If this too does not identify an underlying reason for the bleeding, invasive <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_angiography" title="Cerebral angiography">cerebral angiography</a> could be performed but this requires access to the bloodstream with an intravascular catheter and can cause further strokes as well as complications at the insertion site and this investigation is therefore reserved for specific situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson2015_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson2015-71">[71]</a></sup> If there are symptoms suggesting that the hemorrhage might have occurred as a result of <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_venous_sinus_thrombosis" title="Cerebral venous sinus thrombosis">venous thrombosis</a>, CT or MRI venography can be used to examine the cerebral veins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson2015_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson2015-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Misdiagnosis">Misdiagnosis</span></h3>
<p>Among people with ischemic strokes, misdiagnosis occurs 2 to 26% of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bak2018_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bak2018-72">[72]</a></sup> A "stroke chameleon" (SC) is stroke which is diagnosed as something else.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bak2018_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bak2018-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stroke_chameleons,_Dupre_et._al,_2013_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stroke_chameleons,_Dupre_et._al,_2013-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>People not having a stroke may also be misdiagnosed as a stroke. Giving thrombolytics (clot-busting) in such cases causes intracerebral bleeding 1 to 2% of the time, which is less than that of people with strokes. This unnecessary treatment adds to health care costs. Even so, the AHA/ASA guidelines state that starting intravenous tPA in possible mimics is preferred to delaying treatment for additional testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bak2018_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bak2018-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Women, African-Americans, Hispanic-Americans, Asian and Pacific Islanders are more often misdiagnosed for a condition other than stroke when in fact having a stroke. In addition, adults under 44 years of age are seven times more likely to have a stroke missed than are adults over 75 years of age. This is especially the case for younger people with posterior circulation infarcts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bak2018_72-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bak2018-72">[72]</a></sup> Some medical centers have used hyperacute MRI in experimental studies for persons initially thought to have a low likelihood of stroke. And in some of these persons, strokes have been found which were then treated with thrombolytic medication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bak2018_72-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bak2018-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span></h2>
<p>Given the disease burden of strokes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prevention_(medical)" title="Prevention (medical)">prevention</a> is an important <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> concern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12234233_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12234233-74">[74]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_prevention" title="Primary prevention">Primary prevention</a> is less effective than secondary prevention (as judged by the <a href="/wiki/Number_needed_to_treat" title="Number needed to treat">number needed to treat</a> to prevent one stroke per year).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12234233_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12234233-74">[74]</a></sup> Recent guidelines detail the evidence for primary prevention in stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PreventionGuidelines_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-PreventionGuidelines-75">[75]</a></sup> In those who are otherwise healthy, aspirin does not appear beneficial and thus is not recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In people who have had a myocardial infarction or those with a high cardiovascular risk, it provides some protection against a first stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP01_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP01-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2002_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2002-78">[78]</a></sup> In those who have previously had a stroke, treatment with medications such as <a href="/wiki/Aspirin" title="Aspirin">aspirin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clopidogrel" title="Clopidogrel">clopidogrel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dipyridamole" title="Dipyridamole">dipyridamole</a> may be beneficial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP01_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-AP01-77">[77]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Preventive_Services_Task_Force" title="U.S. Preventive Services Task Force">U.S. Preventive Services Task Force</a> (USPSTF) recommends against <a href="/wiki/Screening_(medicine)" title="Screening (medicine)">screening</a> for <a href="/wiki/Carotid_artery_stenosis" title="Carotid artery stenosis">carotid artery stenosis</a> in those without symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk_factors">Risk factors</span></h3>
<p>The most important modifiable risk factors for stroke are high blood pressure and atrial fibrillation although the size of the effect is small; 833 people have to be treated for 1 year to prevent one stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Other modifiable risk factors include high blood cholesterol levels, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes mellitus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/End-stage_kidney_disease" title="End-stage kidney disease">end-stage kidney disease</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hu2018_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hu2018-7">[7]</a></sup> cigarette smoking<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid7596004_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid7596004-83">[83]</a></sup> (active and passive), heavy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcohol_consumption_and_health" title="Alcohol consumption and health">alcohol</a> use,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12578491_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12578491-84">[84]</a></sup> drug use,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid1891081_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid1891081-85">[85]</a></sup> lack of <a href="/wiki/Physical_activity" title="Physical activity">physical activity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a>, processed <a href="/wiki/Red_meat" title="Red meat">red meat</a> consumption,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> and unhealthy diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-American_heart_risk_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-American_heart_risk-87">[87]</a></sup> Smoking just one cigarette per day increases the risk more than 30%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Alcohol use could predispose to ischemic stroke, as well as intracerebral and subarachnoid hemorrhage via multiple mechanisms (for example, via hypertension, atrial fibrillation, rebound <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thrombocytosis" title="Thrombocytosis">thrombocytosis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platelet_aggregation" title="Platelet aggregation">platelet aggregation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clotting" title="Clotting">clotting</a> disturbances).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid3810763_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid3810763-89">[89]</a></sup> Drugs, most commonly amphetamines and cocaine, can induce stroke through damage to the blood vessels in the brain and acute hypertension.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrison's_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison's-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17404126_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17404126-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Migraine" title="Migraine">Migraine</a> with <a href="/wiki/Aura_(symptom)" title="Aura (symptom)">aura</a> doubles a person's risk for ischemic stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stroke2009_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stroke2009-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Untreated, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celiac_disease" title="Celiac disease">celiac disease</a> regardless of the presence of symptoms can be an underlying cause of stroke, both in children and adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CiaccioLewis2017_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-CiaccioLewis2017-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>High levels of physical activity reduce the risk of stroke by about 26%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2016_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2016-94">[94]</a></sup> There is a lack of high quality studies looking at promotional efforts to improve lifestyle factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14705756_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid14705756-95">[95]</a></sup> Nonetheless, given the large body of circumstantial evidence, best medical management for stroke includes advice on diet, exercise, smoking and alcohol use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endart-review_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-endart-review-96">[96]</a></sup> Medication is the most common method of stroke prevention; <a href="/wiki/Carotid_endarterectomy" title="Carotid endarterectomy">carotid endarterectomy</a> can be a useful surgical method of preventing stroke.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood_pressure">Blood pressure</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">High blood pressure</a> accounts for 35–50% of stroke risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8614485_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid8614485-97">[97]</a></sup> Blood pressure reduction of 10 mmHg systolic or 5 mmHg diastolic reduces the risk of stroke by ~40%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Lowering blood pressure has been conclusively shown to prevent both ischemic and hemorrhagic strokes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Psaty2003_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Psaty2003-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8551820_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid8551820-100">[100]</a></sup> It is equally important in secondary prevention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9412649_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9412649-101">[101]</a></sup> Even people older than 80 years and those with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isolated_systolic_hypertension" title="Isolated systolic hypertension">isolated systolic hypertension</a> benefit from antihypertensive therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-INDANAsub_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-INDANAsub-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Staessen2000_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Staessen2000-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HYVET-NEJM2008_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-HYVET-NEJM2008-104">[104]</a></sup> The available evidence does not show large differences in stroke prevention between antihypertensive drugs—therefore, other factors such as protection against other forms of cardiovascular disease and cost should be considered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BPLT_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-BPLT-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12479763_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12479763-106">[106]</a></sup> The routine use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta-blocker" title="Beta-blocker">beta-blockers</a> following a stroke or TIA has not been shown to result in benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood_lipids">Blood lipids</span></h4>
<p>High cholesterol levels have been inconsistently associated with (ischemic) stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8551820_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid8551820-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM1989_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-NEJM1989-108">[108]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statins" title="Statins">Statins</a> have been shown to reduce the risk of stroke by about 15%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Statins2008_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Statins2008-109">[109]</a></sup> Since earlier meta-analyses of other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipid-lowering_drugs" title="Lipid-lowering drugs">lipid-lowering drugs</a> did not show a decreased risk,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid7802520_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid7802520-110">[110]</a></sup> statins might exert their effect through mechanisms other than their lipid-lowering effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Statins2008_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Statins2008-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Diabetes_mellitus">Diabetes mellitus</span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">Diabetes mellitus</a> increases the risk of stroke by 2 to 3 times.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> While intensive blood sugar control has been shown to reduce small blood vessel complications such as <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_nephropathy" title="Diabetic nephropathy">kidney damage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_retinopathy" title="Diabetic retinopathy">damage to the retina of the eye</a> it has not been shown to reduce large blood vessel complications such as stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UKPDS_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-UKPDS-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ProACTIVE_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-ProACTIVE-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anticoagulation_drugs">Anticoagulation drugs</span></h4>
<p>Oral anticoagulants such as <a href="/wiki/Warfarin" title="Warfarin">warfarin</a> have been the mainstay of stroke prevention for over 50 years. However, several studies have shown that aspirin and other <a href="/wiki/Antiplatelet_drug" title="Antiplatelet drug">antiplatelets</a> are highly effective in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_prevention" title="Secondary prevention">secondary prevention</a> after a stroke or transient ischemic attack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP01_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-AP01-77">[77]</a></sup> Low doses of aspirin (for example 75–150 mg) are as effective as high doses but have fewer side effects; the lowest effective dose remains unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10371234_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10371234-113">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thienopyridine" title="Thienopyridine">Thienopyridines</a> (<a href="/wiki/Clopidogrel" title="Clopidogrel">clopidogrel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ticlopidine" title="Ticlopidine">ticlopidine</a>) might be slightly more effective than aspirin and have a decreased risk of <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_bleeding" title="Gastrointestinal bleeding">gastrointestinal bleeding</a>, but are more expensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CochraneThienopyridines_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-CochraneThienopyridines-114">[114]</a></sup> Both aspirin and clopidogrel may be useful in the first few weeks after a minor stroke or high risk TIA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Clopidogrel has less side effects than ticlopidine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CochraneThienopyridines_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-CochraneThienopyridines-114">[114]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dipyridamole" title="Dipyridamole">Dipyridamole</a> can be added to aspirin therapy to provide a small additional benefit, even though headache is a common side effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESPRIT_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESPRIT-116">[116]</a></sup> Low-dose aspirin is also effective for stroke prevention after having a myocardial infarction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2002_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2002-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Those with <a href="/wiki/Atrial_fibrillation" title="Atrial fibrillation">atrial fibrillation</a> have a 5% a year risk of stroke, and this risk is higher in those with valvular atrial fibrillation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFIB_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-AFIB-117">[117]</a></sup> Depending on the stroke risk, anticoagulation with medications such as <a href="/wiki/Warfarin" title="Warfarin">warfarin</a> or aspirin is useful for prevention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFib-guidelines_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-AFib-guidelines-118">[118]</a></sup> Except in people with atrial fibrillation, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oral_anticoagulants" title="Oral anticoagulants">oral anticoagulants</a> are not advised for stroke prevention—any benefit is offset by bleeding risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17239798_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17239798-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>In primary prevention, however, antiplatelet drugs did not reduce the risk of ischemic stroke but increased the risk of major bleeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aspirin_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-aspirin-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aspirin-meta_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-aspirin-meta-121">[121]</a></sup> Further studies are needed to investigate a possible protective effect of aspirin against ischemic stroke in women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aspirin-women_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-aspirin-women-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gender_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-gender-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Surgery">Surgery</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carotid_endarterectomy" title="Carotid endarterectomy">Carotid endarterectomy</a> or carotid <a href="/wiki/Angioplasty" title="Angioplasty">angioplasty</a> can be used to remove atherosclerotic narrowing of the <a href="/wiki/Carotid_artery" title="Carotid artery">carotid artery</a>. There is evidence supporting this procedure in selected cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endart-review_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-endart-review-96">[96]</a></sup> Endarterectomy for a significant stenosis has been shown to be useful in preventing further strokes in those who have already had one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12531577_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12531577-124">[124]</a></sup> Carotid artery stenting has not been shown to be equally useful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endarterectomy-meta_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-endarterectomy-meta-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> People are selected for surgery based on age, gender, degree of stenosis, time since symptoms and the person's preferences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endart-review_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-endart-review-96">[96]</a></sup> Surgery is most efficient when not delayed too long—the risk of recurrent stroke in a person who has a 50% or greater stenosis is up to 20% after 5 years, but endarterectomy reduces this risk to around 5%. The number of procedures needed to cure one person was 5 for early surgery (within two weeks after the initial stroke), but 125 if delayed longer than 12 weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15043958_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15043958-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16087900_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16087900-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Screening_(medicine)" title="Screening (medicine)">Screening</a> for carotid artery narrowing has not been shown to be a useful test in the general population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USPSTF-carotidUS_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-USPSTF-carotidUS-129">[129]</a></sup> Studies of surgical intervention for carotid artery stenosis without symptoms have shown only a small decrease in the risk of stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15135594_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15135594-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16235289_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16235289-131">[131]</a></sup> To be beneficial, the complication rate of the surgery should be kept below 4%. Even then, for 100 surgeries, 5 people will benefit by avoiding stroke, 3 will develop stroke despite surgery, 3 will develop stroke or die due to the surgery itself, and 89 will remain stroke-free but would also have done so without intervention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endart-review_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-endart-review-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">Diet</span></h4>
<p>Nutrition, specifically the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_diet" title="Mediterranean diet">Mediterranean-style diet</a>, has the potential for decreasing the risk of having a stroke by more than half.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strokenutrition_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-strokenutrition-132">[132]</a></sup> It does not appear that lowering levels of <a href="/wiki/Homocysteine" title="Homocysteine">homocysteine</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folic_acid" title="Folic acid">folic acid</a> affects the risk of stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="24" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="CDC_video_about_stroke_2.ogv" height="124" id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/CDC_video_about_stroke_2.ogv/220px--CDC_video_about_stroke_2.ogv.jpg" preload="none" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="1326985" data-framerate="29.97002997003" data-height="720" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file, 1,280 × 720 (1.33 Mbps)" data-width="1280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f7/CDC_video_about_stroke_2.ogv" type='video/ogg; codecs="theora"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CDC_video_about_stroke_2.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> public service announcement about a woman having a stroke after pregnancy.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women">Women</span></h3>
<p>A number of specific recommendations have been made for women including taking aspirin after the 11th week of pregnancy if there is a history of previous chronic high blood pressure and taking blood pressure medications during pregnancy if the blood pressure is greater than 150 mmHg systolic or greater than 100 mmHg diastolic. In those who have previously had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preeclampsia" title="Preeclampsia">preeclampsia</a> other risk factors should be treated more aggressively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bush2014_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bush2014-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Previous_stroke_or_TIA">Previous stroke or TIA</span></h3>
<p>Keeping blood pressure below 140/90 mmHg is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHA2014_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHA2014-136">[136]</a></sup> Anticoagulation can prevent recurrent ischemic strokes. Among people with nonvalvular atrial fibrillation, anticoagulation can reduce stroke by 60% while antiplatelet agents can reduce stroke by 20%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17577005_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17577005-137">[137]</a></sup> However, a recent meta-analysis suggests harm from anticoagulation started early after an embolic stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17204681_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17204681-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Stroke prevention treatment for atrial fibrillation is determined according to the <a href="/wiki/CHA2DS2%E2%80%93VASc_score" title="CHA2DS2–VASc score">CHA2DS2–VASc score</a>. The most widely used anticoagulant to prevent thromboembolic stroke in people with nonvalvular atrial fibrillation is the oral agent <a href="/wiki/Warfarin" title="Warfarin">warfarin</a> while a number of newer agents including <a href="/wiki/Dabigatran" title="Dabigatran">dabigatran</a> are alternatives which do not require <a href="/wiki/Prothrombin_time" title="Prothrombin time">prothrombin time</a> monitoring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHA2014_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-AHA2014-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>Anticoagulants, when used following stroke, should not be stopped for dental procedures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>If studies show carotid artery stenosis, and the person has a degree of residual function on the affected side, carotid endarterectomy (surgical removal of the stenosis) may decrease the risk of recurrence if performed rapidly after stroke.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ischemic_stroke">Ischemic stroke</span></h3>
<p>Aspirin reduces the overall risk of recurrence by 13% with greater benefit early on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Definitive therapy within the first few hours is aimed at removing the blockage by breaking the clot down (<a href="/wiki/Thrombolysis" title="Thrombolysis">thrombolysis</a>), or by removing it mechanically (<a href="/wiki/Thrombectomy" title="Thrombectomy">thrombectomy</a>). The philosophical premise underlying the importance of rapid stroke intervention was summed up as <i>Time is Brain!</i> in the early 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Years later, that same idea, that rapid cerebral blood flow restoration results in fewer brain cells dying, has been proved and quantified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tight blood sugar control in the first few hours does not improve outcomes and may cause harm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> High blood pressure is also not typically lowered as this has not been found to be helpful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cerebrolysin" title="Cerebrolysin">Cerebrolysin</a>, a mixture of pig-derived neurotrophic factors used to treat acute ischemic stroke in many Asian and European countries, does not improve outcomes and may increase the risk of severe adverse events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Thrombolysis">Thrombolysis</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Thrombolysis" title="Thrombolysis">Thrombolysis</a>, such as with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recombinant_tissue_plasminogen_activator" title="Recombinant tissue plasminogen activator">recombinant tissue plasminogen activator</a> (rtPA), in acute ischemic stroke, when given within three hours of symptom onset, results in an overall benefit of 10% with respect to living without disability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward2014_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward2014-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2014_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2014-149">[149]</a></sup> It does not, however, improve chances of survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward2014_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ward2014-148">[148]</a></sup> Benefit is greater the earlier it is used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward2014_148-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ward2014-148">[148]</a></sup> Between three and four and a half hours the effects are less clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dyna2014_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dyna2014-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> The AHA/ASA recommend it for certain people in this time frame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> A 2014 review found a 5% increase in the number of people living without disability at three to six months; however, there was a 2% increased risk of death in the short term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2014_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2014-149">[149]</a></sup> After four and a half hours thrombolysis worsens outcomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dyna2014_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dyna2014-150">[150]</a></sup> These benefits or lack of benefits occurred regardless of the age of the person treated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2012_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2012-154">[154]</a></sup> There is no reliable way to determine who will have an intracranial bleed post-treatment versus who will not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> In those with findings of savable tissue on medical imaging between 4.5 hours and 9 hours or who wake up with a stroke, alteplase results in some benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>Its use is endorsed by the <a href="/wiki/American_Heart_Association" title="American Heart Association">American Heart Association</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_College_of_Emergency_Physicians" title="American College of Emergency Physicians">American College of Emergency Physicians</a> and the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Neurology" title="American Academy of Neurology">American Academy of Neurology</a> as the recommended treatment for acute stroke within three hours of onset of symptoms as long as there are no other contraindications (such as abnormal lab values, high blood pressure, or recent surgery). This position for tPA is based upon the findings of two studies by one group of investigators<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> which showed that tPA improves the chances for a good neurological outcome. When administered within the first three hours thrombolysis improves functional outcome without affecting mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> 6.4% of people with large strokes developed substantial brain bleeding as a complication from being given tPA thus part of the reason for increased short term mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dubinsky06_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dubinsky06-159">[159]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Emergency_Medicine" title="American Academy of Emergency Medicine">American Academy of Emergency Medicine</a> had previously stated that objective evidence regarding the applicability of tPA for acute ischemic stroke was insufficient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> In 2013 the American College of Emergency Medicine refuted this position,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> acknowledging the body of evidence for the use of tPA in ischemic stroke;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> but debate continues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intra-arterial_fibrinolysis" title="Intra-arterial fibrinolysis">Intra-arterial fibrinolysis</a>, where a catheter is passed up an artery into the brain and the medication is injected at the site of thrombosis, has been found to improve outcomes in people with acute ischemic stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Endovascular_treatment">Endovascular treatment</span></h4>
<p>Mechanical removal of the blood clot causing the ischemic stroke, called <a href="/wiki/Thrombectomy" title="Thrombectomy">mechanical thrombectomy</a>, is a potential treatment for occlusion of a large artery, such as the <a href="/wiki/Middle_cerebral_artery" title="Middle cerebral artery">middle cerebral artery</a>. In 2015, one review demonstrated the safety and efficacy of this procedure if performed within 12 hours of the onset of symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> It did not change the risk of death, but reduced disability compared to the use of intravenous thrombolysis which is generally used in people evaluated for mechanical thrombectomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> Certain cases may benefit from thrombectomy up to 24 hours after the onset of symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Craniectomy">Craniectomy</span></h4>
<p>Strokes affecting large portions of the brain can cause significant brain swelling with secondary brain injury in surrounding tissue. This phenomenon is mainly encountered in strokes affecting brain tissue dependent upon the middle cerebral artery for blood supply and is also called "malignant cerebral infarction" because it carries a dismal prognosis. Relief of the pressure may be attempted with medication, but some require <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemicraniectomy" title="Hemicraniectomy">hemicraniectomy</a>, the temporary surgical removal of the skull on one side of the head. This decreases the risk of death, although some people – who would otherwise have died – survive with disability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21190097_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21190097-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hemorrhagic_stroke">Hemorrhagic stroke</span></h3>
<p>People with <a href="/wiki/Intracerebral_hemorrhage" title="Intracerebral hemorrhage">intracerebral hemorrhage</a> require supportive care, including blood pressure control if required. People are monitored for changes in the level of consciousness, and their blood sugar and oxygenation are kept at optimum levels. Anticoagulants and antithrombotics can make bleeding worse and are generally discontinued (and reversed if possible).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A proportion may benefit from <a href="/wiki/Neurosurgery" title="Neurosurgery">neurosurgical</a> intervention to remove the blood and treat the underlying cause, but this depends on the location and the size of the hemorrhage as well as patient-related factors, and ongoing research is being conducted into the question as to which people with intracerebral hemorrhage may benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Subarachnoid_hemorrhage" title="Subarachnoid hemorrhage">subarachnoid hemorrhage</a>, early treatment for underlying cerebral aneurysms may reduce the risk of further hemorrhages. Depending on the site of the aneurysm this may be by <a href="/wiki/Craniotomy" title="Craniotomy">surgery that involves opening the skull</a> or <a href="/wiki/Interventional_neuroradiology" title="Interventional neuroradiology">endovascularly</a> (through the blood vessels).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stroke_unit">Stroke unit</span></h3>
<p>Ideally, people who have had a stroke are admitted to a "stroke unit", a ward or dedicated area in a hospital staffed by nurses and therapists with experience in stroke treatment. It has been shown that people admitted to a stroke unit have a higher chance of surviving than those admitted elsewhere in hospital, even if they are being cared for by doctors without experience in stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donnan2008_2-18"><a href="#cite_note-Donnan2008-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nursing_care" title="Nursing care">Nursing care</a> is fundamental in maintaining <a href="/wiki/Skin" title="Skin">skin care</a>, feeding, hydration, positioning, and monitoring <a href="/wiki/Vital_signs" title="Vital signs">vital signs</a> such as temperature, pulse, and blood pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rehabilitation">Rehabilitation</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stroke_rehabilitation" title="Stroke rehabilitation">Stroke rehabilitation</a> is the process by which those with disabling strokes undergo treatment to help them return to normal life as much as possible by regaining and relearning the skills of everyday living. It also aims to help the survivor understand and adapt to difficulties, prevent secondary complications, and educate family members to play a supporting role. Stroke rehabilitation should begin almost immediately with a multidisciplinary approach. The rehabilitation team may involve physicians trained in rehabilitation medicine, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurologist" title="Neurologist">neurologists</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinical_pharmacist" title="Clinical pharmacist">clinical pharmacists</a>, nursing staff, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physiotherapist" title="Physiotherapist">physiotherapists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Occupational_therapist" title="Occupational therapist">occupational therapists</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Speech-language_pathology" title="Speech-language pathology">speech-language pathologists</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Orthotist" title="Orthotist">orthotists</a>. Some teams may also include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychologists" title="Psychologists">psychologists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_work" title="Social work">social workers</a>, since at least one-third of affected people manifests <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post_stroke_depression" title="Post stroke depression">post stroke depression</a>. Validated instruments such as the <a href="/wiki/Barthel_scale" title="Barthel scale">Barthel scale</a> may be used to assess the likelihood of a person who has had a stroke being able to manage at home with or without support subsequent to discharge from a hospital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stroke_rehabilitation" title="Stroke rehabilitation">Stroke rehabilitation</a> should be started as quickly as possible and can last anywhere from a few days to over a year. Most return of function is seen in the first few months, and then improvement falls off with the "window" considered officially by <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. state</a> rehabilitation units and others to be closed after six months, with little chance of further improvement.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag needs references to reliable medical sources. (August 2020)">medical citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, some people have reported that they continue to improve for years, regaining and strengthening abilities like writing, walking, running, and talking.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag needs references to reliable medical sources. (August 2020)">medical citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Daily rehabilitation exercises should continue to be part of the daily routine for people who have had a stroke. Complete recovery is unusual but not impossible and most people will improve to some extent: proper diet and exercise are known to help the brain to recover.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spatial_neglect">Spatial neglect</span></h4>
<p>The current body of evidence is uncertain on the efficacy of cognitive rehabilitation for reducing the disabling effects of neglect and increasing independence remains unproven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Longley_Hazelton_Heal_et_al_2021_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Longley_Hazelton_Heal_et_al_2021-178">[178]</a></sup> However, there is limited evidence that cognitive rehabilitation may have an immediate beneficial effect on tests of neglect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Longley_Hazelton_Heal_et_al_2021_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-Longley_Hazelton_Heal_et_al_2021-178">[178]</a></sup> Overall, no rehabilitation approach can be supported by evidence for spatial neglect.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Automobile_driving">Automobile driving</span></h4>
<p>The current body of evidence is uncertain whether the use of rehabilitation can improve on-road driving skills following stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-:14-179">[179]</a></sup> There is limited evidence that training on a driving simulator will improve performance on recognizing road signs after training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-:14-179">[179]</a></sup> The findings are based on low-quality evidence as further research is needed involving large numbers of participants.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Yoga">Yoga</span></h4>
<p>Based on low quality evidence, it is currently uncertain whether yoga has a significant benefit for stroke rehabilitation on measures of quality of life, balance, strength, endurance, pain, and disability scores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:132_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-:132-180">[180]</a></sup> Yoga may reduce anxiety and could be included as part of patient-centred stroke rehabilitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:132_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-:132-180">[180]</a></sup> Further research is needed assessing the benefits and safety of yoga in stroke rehabilitation.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Action_observation_for_upper_limbs">Action observation for upper limbs</span></h4>
<p>The latest scientific evidence indicates that action observation is beneficial in improving upper limb motor function and dependence in activities of daily living in patients with stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borges_Fernandes_et_al_2022_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borges_Fernandes_et_al_2022-181">[181]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="Updated version https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/35930301 (September 2022)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> Thus, action observation therapy is generally associated with better arm and hand function, with no significant adverse events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borges_Fernandes_et_al_2022_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-Borges_Fernandes_et_al_2022-181">[181]</a></sup> The findings are based on low to moderate quality evidence.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cognitive_rehabilitation_for_attention_deficits">Cognitive rehabilitation for attention deficits</span></h4>
<p>The current body of scientific evidence is uncertain on the effectiveness of cognitive rehabilitation for attention deficits in patients following stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-:11-182">[182]</a></sup> While there may be an immediate effect after treatment on attention, the findings are based on low to moderate quality and small number of studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-:11-182">[182]</a></sup> Further research is needed to assess whether the effect can be sustained in day-to-day tasks requiring attention.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Motor_imagery_for_gait_rehabilitation">Motor imagery for gait rehabilitation</span></h4>
<p>The latest evidence supports the short-term benefits of motor imagery (MI) on walking speed in individuals who have had a stroke, in comparison to other therapies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-:10-183">[183]</a></sup> MI does not improve motor function after stroke and does not seem to cause significant adverse events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-:10-183">[183]</a></sup> The findings are based on low-quality evidence as further research is needed to estimate the effect of MI on walking endurance and the dependence on personal assistance.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_and_occupational_therapy">Physical and occupational therapy</span></h4>
<p>Physical and occupational therapy have overlapping areas of expertise; however, physical therapy focuses on joint range of motion and strength by performing exercises and relearning functional tasks such as bed mobility, transferring, walking and other gross motor functions. Physiotherapists can also work with people who have had a stroke to improve awareness and use of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemiplegic" title="Hemiplegic">hemiplegic</a> side. Rehabilitation involves working on the ability to produce strong movements or the ability to perform tasks using normal patterns. Emphasis is often concentrated on functional tasks and people's goals. One example physiotherapists employ to promote <a href="/wiki/Motor_learning" title="Motor learning">motor learning</a> involves <a href="/wiki/Constraint-induced_movement_therapy" title="Constraint-induced movement therapy">constraint-induced movement therapy</a>. Through continuous practice the person relearns to use and adapt the hemiplegic limb during functional activities to create lasting permanent changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> Physical therapy is effective for recovery of function and mobility after stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CD001920_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-CD001920-185">[185]</a></sup> Occupational therapy is involved in training to help relearn everyday activities known as the <a href="/wiki/Activities_of_daily_living" title="Activities of daily living">activities of daily living</a> (ADLs) such as eating, drinking, dressing, bathing, cooking, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexia_(condition)" title="Alexia (condition)">reading</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agraphia" title="Agraphia">writing</a>, and toileting. Approaches to helping people with urinary incontinence include physical therapy, cognitive therapy, and specialized interventions with experienced medical professionals, however, it is not clear how effective these approaches are at improving urinary incontinence following a stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-186">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>Treatment of spasticity related to stroke often involves early mobilizations, commonly performed by a physiotherapist, combined with elongation of spastic muscles and sustained stretching through various different positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSul07_719_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-OSul07_719-40">[40]</a></sup> Gaining initial improvement in range of motion is often achieved through rhythmic rotational patterns associated with the affected limb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSul07_719_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-OSul07_719-40">[40]</a></sup> After full range has been achieved by the therapist, the limb should be positioned in the lengthened positions to prevent against further contractures, skin breakdown, and disuse of the limb with the use of splints or other tools to stabilize the joint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSul07_719_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-OSul07_719-40">[40]</a></sup> Cold in the form of ice wraps or ice packs have been proven to briefly reduce spasticity by temporarily dampening neural firing rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSul07_719_40-4"><a href="#cite_note-OSul07_719-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sensory_stimulation_therapy#Coactivation" title="Sensory stimulation therapy">Electrical stimulation</a> to the antagonist muscles or vibrations has also been used with some success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSul07_719_40-5"><a href="#cite_note-OSul07_719-40">[40]</a></sup> Physical therapy is sometimes suggested for people who experience sexual dysfunction following a stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Interventions_for_age-related_visual_problems_in_patients_with_stroke">Interventions for age-related visual problems in patients with stroke</span></h5>
<p>With the prevalence of vision problems increasing with age in stroke patients, the overall effect of interventions for age-related visual problems is currently uncertain. It is also not sure whether people with stroke respond differently from the general population when treating eye problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Further research in this area is needed as current body of evidence is very low quality.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Speech_and_language_therapy">Speech and language therapy</span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Speech_and_language_therapy" title="Speech and language therapy">Speech and language therapy</a> is appropriate for people with the speech production disorders: <a href="/wiki/Dysarthria" title="Dysarthria">dysarthria</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mackenzie_2011_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mackenzie_2011-189">[189]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Apraxia_of_speech" title="Apraxia of speech">apraxia of speech</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-West_2005_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-West_2005-190">[190]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aphasia" title="Aphasia">aphasia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> cognitive-communication impairments, and <a href="/wiki/Dysphagia" title="Dysphagia">problems with swallowing</a>. Speech and language therapy for aphasia following stroke compared to no therapy improves functional communication, reading, writing and expressive language. There may be benefit in high intensity and high doses over a longer period, but these higher intensity doses may not be acceptable to everyone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CD001920_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-CD001920-185">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>People who have had a stroke may have particular problems, such as <a href="/wiki/Dysphagia" title="Dysphagia">dysphagia</a>, which can cause swallowed material to pass into the lungs and cause <a href="/wiki/Aspiration_pneumonia" title="Aspiration pneumonia">aspiration pneumonia</a>. The condition may improve with time, but in the interim, a <a href="/wiki/Nasogastric_intubation" title="Nasogastric intubation">nasogastric tube</a> may be inserted, enabling liquid food to be given directly into the stomach. If swallowing is still deemed unsafe, then a <a href="/wiki/Percutaneous_endoscopic_gastrostomy" title="Percutaneous endoscopic gastrostomy">percutaneous endoscopic gastrostomy</a> (PEG) tube is passed and this can remain indefinitely. Swallowing therapy has mixed results as of 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Devices"><a href="/wiki/Orthotics#Ankle–foot_orthosis_(AFO)" title="Orthotics">Devices</a></span></h4>
<p>Often, <a href="/wiki/Assistive_technology" title="Assistive technology">assistive technology</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wheelchairs" title="Wheelchairs">wheelchairs</a>, walkers and canes may be beneficial. Many mobility problems can be improved by the use of <a href="/wiki/Orthotics#Ankle–foot_orthosis_(AFO)" title="Orthotics">ankle foot orthoses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_fitness">Physical fitness</span></h4>
<p>A stroke can also reduce people's general fitness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-194">[194]</a></sup> Reduced fitness can reduce capacity for rehabilitation as well as general health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IQWiG-Fitness_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-IQWiG-Fitness-195">[195]</a></sup> Physical exercises as part of a rehabilitation program following a stroke appear safe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_194-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-194">[194]</a></sup> Cardiorespiratory fitness training that involves walking in rehabilitation can improve speed, tolerance and independence during walking, and may improve balance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_194-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-194">[194]</a></sup> There are inadequate long-term data about the effects of exercise and training on death, dependence and disability after a stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_194-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-194">[194]</a></sup> The future areas of research may concentrate on the optimal exercise prescription and long-term health benefits of exercise. The effect of physical training on cognition also may be studied further.
</p><p>The ability to walk independently in their community, indoors or outdoors, is important following stroke. Although no negative effects have been reported, it is unclear if outcomes can improve with these walking programs when compared to usual treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_therapy_methods">Other therapy methods</span></h4>
<p>Some current and future therapy methods include the use of <a href="/wiki/Virtual_reality" title="Virtual reality">virtual reality</a> and video games for rehabilitation. These forms of rehabilitation offer potential for motivating people to perform specific therapy tasks that many other forms do not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lange_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lange-197">[197]</a></sup> While virtual reality and interactive video gaming are not more effective than conventional therapy for improving upper limb function, when used in conjunction with usual care these approaches may improve upper limb function and ADL function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-198">[198]</a></sup> There are inadequate data on the effect of virtual reality and interactive video gaming on gait speed, balance, participation and quality of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-198">[198]</a></sup> Many clinics and hospitals are adopting the use of these off-the-shelf devices for exercise, social interaction, and rehabilitation because they are affordable, accessible and can be used within the clinic and home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lange_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lange-197">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mirror_box" title="Mirror box">Mirror therapy</a> is associated with improved motor function of the upper extremity in people who have had a stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other non-invasive rehabilitation methods used to augment physical therapy of motor function in people recovering from a stroke include <a href="/wiki/Transcranial_magnetic_stimulation" title="Transcranial magnetic stimulation">transcranial magnetic stimulation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transcranial_direct-current_stimulation" title="Transcranial direct-current stimulation">transcranial direct-current stimulation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Rehabilitation_robotics" title="Rehabilitation robotics">robotic therapies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> Constraint‐induced movement therapy (CIMT), mental practice, mirror therapy, interventions for sensory impairment, virtual reality and a relatively high dose of repetitive task practice may be effective in improving upper limb function. However, further primary research, specifically of CIMT, mental practice, mirror therapy and virtual reality is needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthotics">Orthotics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gehen_mit_Orthese_nach_Schlaganfall_220.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="459" data-file-width="220" decoding="async" height="459" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4c/Gehen_mit_Orthese_nach_Schlaganfall_220.jpg" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gehen_mit_Orthese_nach_Schlaganfall_220.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Walking with an <a href="/wiki/Orthotics#Definition_of_the_orthotic_functions_in_the_case_of_paralysis_after_a_stroke" title="Orthotics">orthosis after a stroke</a></div></div></div>
<p>Clinical studies confirm the importance of <a href="/wiki/Orthotics" title="Orthotics">orthoses</a> in stroke rehabilitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> The orthosis supports the therapeutic applications and also helps to mobilize the patient at an early stage. With the help of an orthosis, physiological standing and walking can be learned again, and late health consequences caused by a wrong gait pattern can be prevented. A treatment with an orthosis can therefore be used to support the therapy.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-management">Self-management</span></h3>
<p>A stroke can affect the ability to live independently and with quality. Self-management programs are a special training that educates stroke survivors about stroke and its consequences, helps them acquire skills to cope with their challenges, and helps them set and meet their own goals during their recovery process. These programs are tailored to the target audience, and led by someone trained and expert in stroke and its consequences (most commonly professionals, but also stroke survivors and peers). A 2016 review reported that these programs improve the quality of life after stroke, without negative effects. People with stroke felt more empowered, happy and satisfied with life after participating in this training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-206">[206]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span></h2>
<p>Disability affects 75% of stroke survivors enough to decrease their ability to work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coffey2000_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coffey2000-207">[207]</a></sup>
Stroke can affect people physically, mentally, emotionally, or a combination of the three. The results of stroke vary widely depending on size and location of the lesion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanford2005_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford2005-208">[208]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_effects">Physical effects</span></h3>
<p>Some of the physical disabilities that can result from stroke include muscle weakness, numbness, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressure_sore" title="Pressure sore">pressure sores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urinary_incontinence" title="Urinary incontinence">incontinence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apraxia" title="Apraxia">apraxia</a> (inability to perform learned movements), difficulties carrying out <a href="/wiki/Activities_of_daily_living" title="Activities of daily living">daily activities</a>, appetite loss, <a href="/wiki/Aphasia" title="Aphasia">speech loss</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vision_loss" title="Vision loss">vision loss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">pain</a>. If the stroke is severe enough, or in a certain location such as parts of the brainstem, <a href="/wiki/Coma" title="Coma">coma</a> or death can result. Up to 10% of people following a stroke develop <a href="/wiki/Seizure" title="Seizure">seizures</a>, most commonly in the week subsequent to the event; the severity of the stroke increases the likelihood of a seizure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reith1997_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reith1997-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burn1997_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burn1997-210">[210]</a></sup> An estimated 15% of people experience urinary incontinence for more than a year following a stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-186">[186]</a></sup> 50% of people have a decline in sexual function (<a href="/wiki/Sexual_dysfunction" title="Sexual dysfunction">sexual dysfunction</a>) following a stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emotional_and_mental_effects">Emotional and mental effects</span></h3>
<p>Emotional and mental dysfunctions correspond to areas in the brain that have been damaged. Emotional problems following a stroke can be due to direct damage to emotional centers in the brain or from frustration and difficulty adapting to new limitations. Post-stroke emotional difficulties include <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>, <a href="/wiki/Panic_attack" title="Panic attack">panic attacks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flat_affect" title="Flat affect">flat affect</a> (failure to express emotions), <a href="/wiki/Mania" title="Mania">mania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apathy" title="Apathy">apathy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychosis" title="Psychosis">psychosis</a>. Other difficulties may include a decreased ability to communicate emotions through facial expression, body language and voice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p>Disruption in self-identity, relationships with others, and emotional well-being can lead to social consequences after stroke due to the lack of ability to communicate. Many people who experience communication impairments after a stroke find it more difficult to cope with the social issues rather than physical impairments. Broader aspects of care must address the emotional impact speech impairment has on those who experience difficulties with speech after a stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mackenzie_2011_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mackenzie_2011-189">[189]</a></sup> Those who experience a stroke are at risk of <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">paralysis</a> which could result in a self disturbed body image which may also lead to other social issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup>
</p><p>30 to 50% of stroke survivors develop post-stroke depression, which is characterized by lethargy, irritability, <a href="/wiki/Sleep_disorder" title="Sleep disorder">sleep disturbances</a>, lowered <a href="/wiki/Self-esteem" title="Self-esteem">self-esteem</a> and withdrawal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Senelick1994_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Senelick1994-213">[213]</a></sup>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinical_depression" title="Clinical depression">Depression</a> can reduce motivation and worsen outcome, but can be treated with social and family support, <a href="/wiki/Psychotherapy" title="Psychotherapy">psychotherapy</a> and, in severe cases, <a href="/wiki/Antidepressant" title="Antidepressant">antidepressants</a>. Psychotherapy sessions may have a small effect on improving mood and preventing depression after a stroke,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> however psychotherapy does not appear to be effective at treating depression after a stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-215">[215]</a></sup> Antidepressant medications may be useful for treating depression after a stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-215">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Emotional_lability" title="Emotional lability">Emotional lability</a>, another consequence of stroke, causes the person to switch quickly between emotional highs and lows and to express emotions inappropriately, for instance with an excess of laughing or crying with little or no provocation. While these expressions of emotion usually correspond to the person's actual emotions, a more severe form of emotional lability causes the affected person to laugh and cry pathologically, without regard to context or emotion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coffey2000_207-1"><a href="#cite_note-Coffey2000-207">[207]</a></sup> Some people show the opposite of what they feel, for example crying when they are happy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Villarosa1993_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Villarosa1993-216">[216]</a></sup> Emotional lability occurs in about 20% of those who have had a stroke. Those with a right hemisphere stroke are more likely to have an empathy problems which can make communication harder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cognitive deficits resulting from stroke include perceptual disorders, <a href="/wiki/Aphasia" title="Aphasia">aphasia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamilton_2011_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton_2011-218">[218]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dementia" title="Dementia">dementia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leys_2005_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leys_2005-219">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> and problems with attention<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coulthard_2006_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coulthard_2006-221">[221]</a></sup> and memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lim_2009_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lim_2009-222">[222]</a></sup> A stroke survivor may be unaware of their own disabilities, a condition called <a href="/wiki/Anosognosia" title="Anosognosia">anosognosia</a>. In a condition called <a href="/wiki/Hemispatial_neglect" title="Hemispatial neglect">hemispatial neglect</a>, the affected person is unable to attend to anything on the side of space opposite to the damaged hemisphere. Cognitive and psychological outcome after a stroke can be affected by the age at which the stroke happened, pre-stroke baseline intellectual functioning, psychiatric history and whether there is pre-existing brain pathology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span></h2>
